data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D58356%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DPin%2BMa%252C%2BXue%2BLiang%2BLi%252C%2BYumeng%2BShi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D50%26contentID%3Dacsami.3c12984%26title%3DCo4S3%2BNanoparticles%2BConfined%2Bin%2Ban%2BMnS%2BNanorod-Grafted%2BN%252C%2BS-Codoped%2BCarbon%2BPolyhedron%2Bfor%2BHighly%2BEfficient%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D58366%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2023" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/medium/am3c12984_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12984&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) suffer from limited ion diffusion and structural expansion, generating the urgent demand for Na<sup>+</sup> accommodable materials with promising architectures. In this work, the rational exploration for Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles confined in an MnS nanorod-grafted N, S-codoped carbon polyhedron (Co–Mn–S@N–S–C) is achieved by the in situ growth of MOF on MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorod along with the subsequent carbonization and sulfurization. Benefiting from the distinctive nanostructure, the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C anode delivers excellent structural stability, resulting in prolonged cycling stability with a capacity retention of 90.2% after 1000 cycles at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, the reaction storage mechanism is clarified by the in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) measurements. The results indicate that properly designed electrode materials have huge potential applications for highly efficient energy storage devices.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D58356%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DPin%2BMa%252C%2BXue%2BLiang%2BLi%252C%2BYumeng%2BShi%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D15%26issueNum%3D50%26contentID%3Dacsami.3c12984%26title%3DCo4S3%2BNanoparticles%2BConfined%2Bin%2Ban%2BMnS%2BNanorod-Grafted%2BN%252C%2BS-Codoped%2BCarbon%2BPolyhedron%2Bfor%2BHighly%2BEfficient%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D58366%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2023" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2023 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291484&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffraction" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Diffraction" class="article__tags__link">Diffraction</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291129&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanorods" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanorods" class="article__tags__link">Nanorods</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transition%20metals" class="article__tags__link">Transition metals</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292275&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transmission electron microscopy" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transmission%20electron%20microscopy" class="article__tags__link">Transmission electron microscopy</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode">anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20battery">sodium-ion battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Nanorod&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_nanorod">nanorod</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Carbonization&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_carbonization">carbonization</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sulfurization&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sulfurization">sulfurization</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been considered attractive candidates for efficient electrical energy storage systems due to the widespread availability and low cost of sodium as well as similar physical/chemical characteristics to lithium-ion batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 1 to 5">(1−5)</a> In the past decade, various anode materials such as carbonaceous materials, transition metal chalcogenides, and alloying compounds have been developed for SIBs. However, the large radius (1.02 Å) and the heavy molar mass (22.99 g mol<sup>–1</sup>) of sodium ions lead to limited ion diffusion and structural expansion, giving rise to the capacity fading of SIBs, which restricts their commercial application. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 6 to 9">(6−9)</a> Therefore, there is an urgent demand to explore novel electrode materials and special design methods to address this challenge.</div><div class="NLM_p">Among the masses of advanced anode materials, metal sulfides hold great promise as high-capacity anodes on account of their multistep reaction mechanisms. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 to 13">(10−13)</a> MnS, for example, is particularly appealing to SIBs because of their low cost, high abundance, and large theoretical specific capacity (616 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Nonetheless, they also have some drawbacks, such as low conductivity and ion-diffusion coefficient, severe volume change, and poor reversibility after deep discharging, which degrade the cycle stability and rate performance of batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 15 to 17">(15−17)</a> Generally, several strategies have been proposed to address the above-mentioned obstacles, including nanostructure designing <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 18 to 21">(18−21)</a> and carbon modification. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 22 to 25">(22−25)</a> For instance, Liu et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> constructed MnS nanowires embedded in carbon nanoreactors to shorten the ion transport pathway and accommodate volume change simultaneously, leading to the high specific capacities of 720 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.02 A g<sup>–1</sup> for SIBs. Zhu et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> successfully prepared the N, S-codoped carbon-coated MnS nanocubes with a core/shell structure. They confirmed the important role of the in situ generated C–S–Mn bonds in enhancing Na-storage performance. As a result, this unique structure exhibited an excellent reversible capacity of 594.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Additionally, compared with the monometal sulfides, using mixed metal sulfides to construct the heterogeneous structure has been demonstrated as an efficient way to enhance the electrochemical performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 27 to 30">(27−30)</a> The heterostructures could combine the advantages of different phase structures of materials to facilitate the electron/charge-transfer and electrochemical reaction kinetics. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 31 and 32">(31,32)</a> To date, many new heterostructures have been explored and used as anode for SIBs, such as Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/FeS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Fe<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>S/MoS<sub>2</sub>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> and Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>/MoS<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> Despite the excellent work that has been published, the improvement of the sodium storage properties is limited. It is believed that combining the above tactics to develop a material with a unique structure can effectively improve the storage performance of sodium, but it is still a big challenge.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this article, we report a well-designed material with Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles confined in a MnS nanorod-grafted N, S-codoped carbon polyhedron (denoted as Co–Mn–S@N–S–C) as the anode material for SIBs. The Co–Mn–S@N–S–C sample was obtained by the in situ growth of ZIF-67 on a MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorod and subsequent carbonization and sulfurization, where the inner one-dimensional (1D) MnS nanorods are grafted with an outer three-dimensional (3D) N, S-codoped carbon polyhedron and the Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles are self-confined in the carbon polyhedron. This delicate design of the materials combined with the 1D MnS nanorod structure and the confined Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles as well as the porous carbonaceous framework not only facilitates the ion migration with improved electrochemical reaction kinetics but also guarantees the sufficient electrolyte/electrode contact area and abundant Na-storage sites. Moreover, the existence of strong Co–N bonds and C–S–Mn/C–N–Mn bonds support the high structural stability and integrity of materials. Owing to these advantages, the as-prepared material delivers an excellent long-term cycling performance with 90.2% capacity retention over 1000 cycles at a current density of 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C anode shows an outstanding rate performance of approximately 305 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Additionally, the intercalation/conversion reaction mechanism of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C has been deeply identified by in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) observation.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Results and discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a schematically describes the synthetic process of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C materials. First, the MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods are synthesized by hydrothermal method according to the literature, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> which show a typical nanorod morphology with an average diameter/length of 100 nm/2 μm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). Second, the well-defined ZIF-67 templates are in situ grown on MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods after the mixing methanol solution of PVP, MeIM, and cobalt nitrate with the methanol dispersion of MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods at room temperature, obtaining the sugar gourd-shaped MnO<sub>2</sub>/ZIF-67 with the diameter of around 500 nm (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c). The power XRD patterns match well with the JCPDS card of MnO<sub>2</sub> and ZIF-67 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Third, during the subsequent carbonization, MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods and ZIF-67 are transformed into MnO nanorod and Co nanoparticles anchored on the MOF-derived N-doped carbon polyhedron (namely MnO/Co/N–C, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d), respectively, which are also confirmed by XRD patterns shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. Finally, MnO/Co/N–C is converted to Co–Mn–S@N–S–C via a sulfurization process, where the MnO nanorods and Co nanoparticles are sulfurized to MnS nanorods and Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles, respectively, together with S doping around the carbon polyhedron. It can be noted that the sugar gourd shape is well-maintained without any obvious separation and damage (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e), demonstrating the structural stability of the materials.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/medium/am3c12984_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis process of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C. SEM images of (b) MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods, (c) MnO<sub>2</sub>/ZIF-67, (d) MnO/Co/N–C, and (e) Co–Mn–S@N–S–C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12984&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The crystal structure of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C was then studied by XRD patterns. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, the distinct diffraction peaks at 29.6, 34.3, 49.3, 61.4, and 72.3° are ascribed to the (111), (200), (220), (222), and (400) of MnS, which well matched the JCPDS card no. 06–0518. The peaks at 30.6, 35.3, 46.8, 51.9, and 54.2° are attributed to the (100), (101), (102), (003), and (110) of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, matching well with the JCPDS card no. 02–1458. Moreover, no unexpected diffraction peaks for precursors and impurities are observed, indicating the successful conversion of MnO<sub>2</sub>/ZIF-67 to Co–Mn–S@N–S–C. In addition, the morphology and crystalline characteristics of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C are further confirmed by TEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>b,<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2</a>). It demonstrates that the carbon polyhedrons with uniformly distributed Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles are well-grafted on the MnS nanorods. Meanwhile, the MnS nanorods are encapsulated by a thin carbon shell (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2b</a>) due to the modification of PVP, which is favorable for fast ion transport. The size distribution of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, suggesting the average particle size of 15–17 nm. The energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mappings (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d) also indicate that the MnS nanorods are well-grafted by N, S-codoped carbon frameworks and that the Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles are homogeneously distributed on the carbon substrate. In addition, high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e) suggests the clear lattice fringes with measured distances of 2.61 and 3.02 Å, corresponding to the (200) and (111) crystal planes for MnS. In addition, the (003) and (101) crystal planes for Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> are also clearly observed. Moreover, the carbon shell shows the typical characteristics of graphitic layers (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c</a>) due to the high pyrolysis temperature, which is favorable for the transformation of the carbon atoms from the disordered state to the ordered graphitic state. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 37 to 40">(37−40)</a> For comparison, Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS were also prepared by the same procedure without MnO<sub>2</sub> and ZIF-67, respectively. All of the diffraction peaks can be indexed to the Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS card no. 02–1458) and MnS (JCPDS card no. 06–0518) without any impurities (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/medium/am3c12984_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD pattern of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C. (b, c) TEM images of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C. (d) TEM elemental mapping images of all elements contained. (e) HRTEM images of the lattice spacing.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12984&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> lists the Raman spectra of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C to evaluate the quality of the carbon structure, in which the obvious peaks at 1344 and 1586 cm<sup>–1</sup> are attributed to the D-band (disorder carbon) and G-band (graphitic carbon). The ratio of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> is 0.99, indicating the existence of small amounts of graphitic carbon. Further, the carbon content of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C is determined by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>). The weight loss is 33.1%, whereas the residue is a composite of Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Mn<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> based on the XRD patterns (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a>). Therefore, the mass ratio of carbon is calculated as 18.27% (the detailed calculation process is described in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Pore structures of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C, Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C, and MnS are also measured by Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) techniques. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>, the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C presents a BET surface area of 16.7 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and a mesoporous structure with the pore size distributed in the range of 10–40 nm. It should be noted that the pristine MnS nanorods show a very low surface area (8.1 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>). Such an enhanced surface area is ascribed to the incorporation of the carbon matrix on the nanorods, facilitating the adsorption of adequate electrolytes and accelerating mass transport. In addition, Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C possesses the highest surface area (61.2 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), which is mainly due to its outstanding pore structure (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>). By contrast, the relative decrease in the surface area for Co–Mn–S@N–S–C stems from the limited incorporation of porous carbon framework and the construction of the unique hierarchical structure, which are beneficial for easing the reaction with the electrolyte and stabilizing the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer.</div><div class="NLM_p">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) is further carried out to investigate the composition of the as-prepared samples. The survey spectra are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>, which indicates that the Mn/Co/S element ratio of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C is around 0.75:0.53:1. The high-resolution C 1s XPS spectrum of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a) can be deconvoluted into four peaks at 288.8, 286.3, 285.2, and 284.6 eV, corresponding to the C═O, C–O, C–S/C–N, and C–C/C═C bond. This demonstrates the distribution of nitrogen and sulfur in the carbon framework and is consistent with the peaks of C 1s spectra for Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C. The high-resolution N 1s peaks (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b) are mainly deconvoluted into four peaks at 405.1, 401.9, 400.4, and 398.7 eV, which are associated with graphitic N, Co–N, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41,42)</a> pyrrolic N, and pyridinic N, respectively, and have no obvious difference with that of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). For the S 2p spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c), a pair of peaks at higher binding energy (purple color) is attributed to the oxidized sulfur species. The two broad peaks at 164.5 and 163.2 eV correspond to the Mn–S bond, whereas the peaks at 165.3 and 164.1 eV correspond to the Co–S bond, both of which shift to higher binding energies in comparison with that of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C, implying the electron transfer from Co to S. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> Moreover, the peak at 161.8 eV is ascribed to the C–S bond, suggesting sulfur doping in the carbon substrate. In the spectrum of Co 2p (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d), two different chemical states can be observed, where the peaks at lower binding energies correspond to Co<sup>2+</sup> and the peaks at higher energies are assigned to Co<sup>3+</sup>. These peaks are related to the Co–S bond, which is the characteristic peaks of cobalt sulfides. Meanwhile, the Co 2p spectrum also confirms the existence of Co–N bonds, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> indicating the strong coupling interaction between Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and nitrogen-doped carbon framework. In contrast with Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C, it can be clearly seen that the spin-energy separation of Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> changes from 17.4 to 16.5 eV, reflecting the distinct interaction between Co and S and the decrease of the Co<sup>3+</sup> electronic density. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> In addition, for the high-resolution spectrum of Mn 2p, the peaks at binding energies of 654.9 and 641.0 eV are assigned to Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively. Moreover, it gives a peak at 643.9 eV, which is ascribed to the C–S–Mn/C–N–Mn bonds. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> The introduction of these high-strength covalent bonds (C–S–Mn/C–N–Mn bonds and Co–N bonds) would ensure the effective contact between MnS/Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and C during cycling, thus contributing to improving the structural stability and stabilizing the structure. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 48 and 49">(48,49)</a></div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/medium/am3c12984_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. High-resolution XPS spectra. (a) C 1s and (b) N 1s of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C, (c) S 2p of three samples, (d) Co 2p of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C and Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C, and (e) Mn 2p of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C and MnS.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12984&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical properties of the as-prepared electrodes are evaluated in the half cells by using sodium foil as the reference and counter electrodes. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a shows the first five CV curves of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C sample at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage window of 0.01–3.0 V. In the first discharge sweep, many reduction peaks can be detected due to the intercalation/conversion of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and MnS with Na<sup>+</sup> ions accompanied by the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer and the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the carbon skeleton. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> Specifically, the broad peak signals at 1.34 0.5–1.8, and 0.4 V are attributed to the conversion reaction of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and MnS to form the intermediates Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub><i>y</i></sub>MnS to metallic Co and Mn as well as byproduct sodium polysulfides (Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub><i>z</i></sub>). In the subsequent cycles, the peaks caused by the reduction of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C with Na<sup>+</sup> appear at 1.55, 0.97, 0.81, and 0.54 V. In addition, the broad features at 0.53 and 2.52 V in the first anodic sweep can be assigned to the deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> from carbon and the side reaction related to the Cu current collector, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> In addition, the peaks between 1.2 and 2.2 V correspond to the multistep desodiation processes of Na<sub>2</sub>S to Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub><i>y</i></sub>MnS intermediates and final Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and MnS. The detailed electrochemical mechanism will be investigated in the following in situ XRD and TEM sections. Additionally, the CV curves of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a> for comparison. In the first cycle of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10a</a>), two peaks at 0.93 and 0.40 V are observed, which can be attributed to the initial insertion and conversion reaction as well as the generation of SEI film, respectively. In the following cycles, the cathodic peaks shift to 1.55, 1.19, 0.91, and 0.83 V, which is related to the multistep electrochemical reaction for sodiation. As for the anodic cycles, the peaks at 1.56 and 1.72 V are assigned to the reverse conversion reaction from Co and Na<sub>2</sub>S to Co<sub><i>x</i></sub>S, whereas the peak at 2.11 V is ascribed to the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> ions from the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoS structure. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10b</a>, during the first cathodic scan, MnS exhibits three peaks at 1.59 0.89, and 0.38 V. The former two peaks correspond to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into MnS and the conversion reaction of MnS to metallic Mn, respectively, which shift to 1.56 and 0.83 V in subsequent cycles. The latter is related to the formation of SEI owing to the electrolyte decomposition and then disappears in the subsequent cycles. During the corresponding anodic scan, the apparent peak at 1.55 V is assigned to the reverse conversion reaction between Mn and Na<sub>2</sub>S. The broad peaks between 1.8 and 2.5 V may be related to the further removal of the Na<sup>+</sup> process of the multistep deintercalation reaction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref52" aria-label="reference 33 and 52">(33,52)</a></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/medium/am3c12984_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) CV curves of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode for the first five cycles. (b) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycling performance at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate capability at various current densities. (e) Galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode at various current densities. (f) Long-term cycling performance at 2.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (g) CV curves of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C at different scan rates. (h) Relationship between peak current and sweep rate of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C. (i) Capacitive and diffusion-controlled contribution of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C. (j) Normalized contribution ratio of capacitive capacities of three samples at different scan rates. (k) Nyquist plots of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C at various potentials.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12984&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The discharge–charge profiles for the first, second, fifth, 10th, and 50th cycles for three electrodes at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S11</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c demonstrates the corresponding cycling performance and Coulombic efficiencies of the electrodes. Co–Mn–S@N–S–C gives an initial discharge/charge capacity of 810.5/663.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to an initial Coulombic efficiency of 81.9%, which is superior to that of the Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS electrodes (67.3 and 65.5%, respectively). The irreversible capacity loss is mainly ascribed to the formation of the SEI layer on the surface of electrode materials. After five cycles, the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode has a reversible specific capacity of 598.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and a capacity of 566.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> is retained after 50 cycles, corresponding to 94.6% retention of the discharge capacity from the fifth cycle. However, only the capacities of 398.8 and 297.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> are delivered and the capacities of 336.5 and 256.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> are retained after 50 cycles for the Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS electrodes, respectively, which are much lower than that of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C. This demonstrates the better structural stability and higher sodium storage capability for the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode. In addition, an ex situ SEM image after cycling for 50 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>) strongly confirms that the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C almost maintains the structural integrity without obvious damage. The rate performance of electrodes is evaluated at varying current densities ranging from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d. The specific discharge capacities of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C are 671.0, 605.6, 537.0, 483.0, 430.7, 352.1, and 283.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode is again backed to a current rate of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, it resumes a capacity of 649.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, suggesting superior stability for extended cycling. Moreover, the increase of current density does not lead to the huge increase of overpotential (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e) in comparison with that of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS electrode (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>), indicating the outstanding sodium diffusion kinetics of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode. To explore the structural stability, the long cycling performance at a current density of 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> is also evaluated. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f, after 1000 cycles, the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode delivers a specific capacity of 371.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, corresponding to a capacity retention rate of 90.2%. Interestingly, the gradually increasing capacity during cycling can be observed, which may be related to the optimization of stable SEI by a high-rate sodiation-induced reactivation. As a comparison, although such a phenomenon is also seen in pure MnS nanorods, without the incorporation of MnS nanorod, a distinct capacity decay occurs after 1000 cycles with a capacity retention rate of 50.9%, which may be attributed to the pulverization of materials. In addition, without the incorporation of MnS nanorods and the unique hierarchical architecture, the bulk Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C electrode exhibits apparent capacity fading with a capacity retention rate of only 31.1% after 1000 cycles. Therefore, the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode demonstrates the obvious advantages in reversible capacity under long-term cycling.</div><div class="NLM_p">To ascertain the possible reasons for the superior electrochemical performance, scan rate-dependent CV measurements are performed sequentially (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S14</a>). Generally, the relationship between the scan rate (<i>v</i>) and the measured peak current (<i>i</i>) complies with the following equation<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq1"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>a</mi><msup><mi>v</mi><mi>b</mi></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq2"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>log</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace="0.25em">⁡</mo><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>b</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace="0.1em">⁡</mo><mi>log</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace="0.25em">⁡</mo><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><mi>log</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace="0.25em">⁡</mo><mi>a</mi></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"></div></div>where both <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are empirical parameters. According to the previous reports, the value of <i>b</i> of 0.5 stands for the diffusion-controlled behavior, whereas the value of 1.0 indicates the surface-controlled capacitive one. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h, the <i>b</i> values calculated by the fitted line for peaks 1–6 are 0.80, 0.83, 0.75, 0.71, 0.81, and 0.96, respectively, suggesting the surface-controlled capacitive sodiation mechanism for the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode. The ratio of surface-controlled capacitive contribution (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>) and diffusion-controlled contribution (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sub>1/2</sub>) for sodium can be verified by the following relationship<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="ueq3"><div class="equation"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>V</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>1</mn></msub><mi>v</mi><mo>+</mo><msub><mi>k</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><msup><mi>v</mi><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></div><div class="label"></div></div>As identified, the surface capacitive contributions at 1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> for Co–Mn–S@N–S–C, Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C, and MnS electrodes are 86.8% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>i), 82.0% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14e</a>), and 76.9% (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14f</a>), respectively. Moreover, with the increase of the scan rates, the surface capacitive contribution increases (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>), where the fraction of diffusion-controlled contribution decreases (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>j). It has been demonstrated that the higher ratio of surface capacitive contribution is favorable to the stable and fast sodium storage, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref53'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> which directly results in superior long-term cycling stability and rate capability.</div><div class="NLM_p">The in situ electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) during the first charge/discharge process at different potentials is also investigated to clarify the outstanding electrochemical performance of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>k, all Nyquist plots show similar features with the semicircle in the high-frequency region and a line in the low-frequency region, which are related to the charge–discharge resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) and the Warburg impedance (<i>W</i>). It can be observed that the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values are very stable at different voltages, suggesting the fast and robust charge-transfer properties during the successive structure evolution processes. Meanwhile, after 100 cycles, the Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS electrodes present higher <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>), confirming the advantages of the unique structure for Co–Mn–S@N–S–C in greatly promoting the charge-transfer kinetics.</div><div class="NLM_p">The microstructure and morphology evolution for the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode during the charge/discharge processes are further conducted via in situ XRD and TEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>). In situ XRD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a) is measured at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, during which a sequence of continuous XRD patterns over the first charge–discharge cycle is collected. Of note, the peaks at 38.8, 41.5, and 44.2° belong to the characteristic information on BeO (JCPDS card no. 35–0818). The peak at 46.1° is indexed to Be (JCPDS card no. 22–0111). In the XRD contour map, pristine Co–Mn–S@N–S–C shows two prominent strong peaks at 34.5 and 49.6°, corresponding to the (200) and (220) planes of MnS. The weak peaks at 35.4 and 47.0° are assigned to the (101) and (102) planes of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. As sodiation proceeds, the intensity of the peak for Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> gradually weakens until it disappears due to Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> formation. Notably, no obvious signals of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> can be detected by XRD, which is mainly attributed to the weak resulting Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> signal. With continuous discharging, additional peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS card no. 76–1763) emerge at the positions of 35.0 and 49.0°, and the matched crystal planes are (510) and (640), respectively. Particularly, with the formation of Na<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, the peaks for MnS do not disappear but just become weaker, looking thinner in the XRD contour map. This indicates the partial intercalation for MnS in the sodiation reaction. As the Na<sup>+</sup> ions insert further into the electrode, the peaks for Na<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> gradually disappear, whereas the new peaks stemming from the Na polysulfides Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub> <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref54 ref55 ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref55 ref56" aria-label="reference 54 to 56">(54−56)</a> appear around 35 and 48° and then disappear, accompanied by the emergence of the crystalline planes (220) and (311) of Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS card no. 47–1698) at 38.8 and 46.3°, respectively, indicating the conversion reaction between Na<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>/Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup> as well as the formation of metallic Mn and Co. However, no obvious peaks of Mn and Co can be identified, which can be explained by either the poor crystallinity or the ultrasmall crystal size. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57 ref58 ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57 ref58 ref59" aria-label="reference 57 to 59">(57−59)</a> During the desodiation process, the expected reversible conversion reaction occurs, as supported by the disappearance of Na polysulfide peaks together with the re-emergence of Na<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. After that, the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> gradually back to the position of MnS during the subsequent desodiation process, demonstrating the reversible Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation reaction. However, the reformed Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> peaks could not be detected, maybe due to their weak signal.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/medium/am3c12984_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) In situ XRD patterns of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode in a contour plot for the first cycle at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Ex situ HRTEM images of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode at different initial discharged and charged states: (b) discharged state of 1.2 V; (c) discharged state of 0.5 V; (d) discharged state of 0.01 V; (e) charged state of 2.0 V; and (f) charged state of 3.0 V. (g, h) Real-time in situ TEM images of morphology evolution during Na intercalation into electrode and the corresponding SAED patterns (i, j).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/asset/images/large/am3c12984_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.3c12984&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Moreover, the phase transformation of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode during the initial discharge–charge cycle can be verified by the ex situ HRTEM measurements. When the potential drops to 1.2 V, the product of Na<sub>2</sub>CoS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS card no. 79–2145) is produced as confirmed by the clear lattice fringes of 2.72 Å in the HRTEM image of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b, which originated from its (211) crystalline planes. Further decreasing the voltage to 0.5 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c), the <i>d</i>-spacing of 2.21 and 2.35 Å assigned to the (060) and (141) planes of Na<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> and Na<sub>2</sub>CoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively, can be observed. As the electrode is continuously discharged to 0.01 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d), the lattice fringes of Co (JCPDS card no. 88–2325) with the (111) plane, Mn (JCPDS card no. 89–4085) with the (330) plane, and Na<sub>2</sub>S with the (220) plane emerge, demonstrating the generation of the conversion reaction. In addition, the signal of MnS is detected with the <i>d</i>-spacing of 2.61 Å associated with the (200) plane during the whole discharging process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b–<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d), revealing its partial intercalation/conversion reaction with sodium. When the electrode is charged back to 2 V (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e), the lattice fringes of the reversibly transformed phase Na<sub>2</sub>CoS<sub>2</sub> with the (002) facet and Na<sub>2</sub>Mn<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> with the (−131) and (−402) facets are detected again due to sodium extraction. In addition, at the charged state of 3.0 V, only the signals of MnS and Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> can be observed, further suggesting the reversibility of phase transformation. These results are not only consistent with the above in situ XRD results but also provide more intuitive and sufficient evidence for the multistep intercalation and conversion reaction mechanisms of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode with Na<sup>+</sup> during the sodiation/desodiation processes.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Real-time in situ TEM observation is further investigated to explore the morphology and structure evolution upon sodiation reactions. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g,h demonstrates the in situ TEM images of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode connected to Na. The corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>i,j. It can be seen that after 280 s of sodiation, the morphology and size for Co–Mn–S@N–S–C almost do not change, indicating the superior structural stability of materials. The SAED patterns (280 s) reveal the presence of Na<sub>2</sub>S and Mn/Co metal, confirming the conversion reactions. Meanwhile, the lattice fringe with spacing distances of 2.61 Å belongs to the (200) plane of MnS, demonstrating the partial intercalation conversion process for MnS.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, a unique architecture with Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles confined in MnS nanorod-grafted N, S-codoped carbon polyhedron as an anode for sodium-ion batteries is developed. This nanostructure design assimilates the advantages of carbon materials and different dimensional nanomaterials, resulting in highly efficient electrochemical performance. The prepared Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode exhibits a long cycle life (90.2% at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>) and a high surface capacitive contribution (86.8% at 2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>). The in situ EIS, real-time TEM, and ex situ SEM show very few changes, indicating outstanding structural stability during the charge/discharge reactions. In addition, the in situ XRD, ex situ HRTEM, and SAED patterns demonstrate the intercalation conversion mechanism of the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode for sodium storage. Therefore, this work will contribute to the development of SIBs and other energy storage devices for new advanced electrode materials.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">4.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The pristine MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods were prepared by hydrothermal. Thirty milliliters of 0.1 M KMnO<sub>4</sub> was mixed with 30 mL of 0.6 M MnSO<sub>4</sub>·H2O under vigorous stirring. The mixture was then loaded into a 100 mL Teflon-lined autoclave and hydrothermally treated at 140 °C for 12 h. After cooling to room temperature, the sample was collected by centrifugation, washed with distilled water, and dried overnight in air at 80 °C. The obtained MnO<sub>2</sub> nanorods (65.81 mg) were added into 10 mL of methanol dispersed with 0.5628 g of PVP in advance and stirred for 30 min (denoted as solution 1). Next, 0.291 g of Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O was added into 10 mL of methanol to get the uniform solution 2. Then, solution 2 was poured into solution 1 and stirred for 2 h to ensure the complete absorption of Co<sup>2+</sup>. Meanwhile, 0.6568 g of 2-methylimidazole (MeIM) was added to 10 mL of methanol and subsequently dropped into the above solution to in situ get the uniform growth of ZIF-67. After centrifugation and drying, the material of MnO<sub>2</sub>/ZIF-67 was obtained. MnO<sub>2</sub>/ZIF-67 was transferred to a tubular furnace for carbonization at 800 °C for 2 h, obtaining the material for MnO/Co/N–C. Eventually, Co–Mn–S@N–S–C was obtained after sulfurization under 600 °C for 2 h. The other experimental details for the synthesis of materials and the characterization are described in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i9">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c12984?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.3c12984</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Experimental details including materials synthesis and characterization; XRD patterns of MnO<sub>2</sub>/ZIF-67 and MnO/Co/N–C; TEM images of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C; the size distribution of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles; XRD patterns of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS; Raman spectra of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C; TGA images of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C and XRD pattern of TGA residue; the nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms and pore size distribution curves; survey XPS spectra of Co–Mn–S@N–S–C, Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C, and MnS; high-resolution XPS spectra of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C (C 1s, N 1s); CV curves of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS electrodes for the first five cycles; galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS electrodes at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>; ex situ SEM image the Co–Mn–S@N–S–C electrode material before and after 50 cycles; galvanostatic charge–discharge profiles of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS electrodes at different current densities; CV curves of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS at different scan rates; the relationship between peak current and sweep rate of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS; capacitive and diffusion-controlled contribution of Co<sub>4</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@N–S–C and MnS at 2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; the normalized surface capacitive-controlled contribution ratio at different scan rates; and Nyquist plots of three electrodes before and after 100 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.3c12984/suppl_file/am3c12984_si_001.pdf">am3c12984_si_001.pdf (1.33 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.3c12984" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lim1QcSHeL1TQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1038/natrevmats.2018.13"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vaalma, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Buchholz, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weil, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Passerini, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Cost and Resource Analysis of Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nat.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Materials Design for High-Safety Sodium-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Chao; Xin, Sen; Mai, Liqiang; You, Ya</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000974</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADEMBC</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1614-6840</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-Blackwell</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries, with their evident superiority in resource abundance and cost, are emerging as promising next-generation energy storage systems for large-scale applications, such as smart grids and low-speed elec. vehicles.  Accidents related to fires and explosions for batteries are a reminder that safety is prerequisite for energy storage systems, esp. when aiming for grid-scale use.  In a typical electrochem. secondary battery, the elec. power is stored and released via processes that generate thermal energy, leading to temp. increments in the battery system, which is the main cause for battery thermal abuse.  The investigation of the energy generated during the chem./electrochem. reactions is of paramount importance for battery safety, unfortunately, it has not received the attention it deserves.  In this review, the fundamentals of the heat generation, accumulation, and transportation in a battery system are summarized and recent key research on materials design to improve sodium-ion battery safety is highlighted.  Several effective materials design concepts are also discussed.  This review is designed to arouse the attention of researcher and scholars and inspire further improvements in battery safety.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqhWwPcmTnIjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lim1QcSHeL1TQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXptlKqu7g%253D&amp;md5=965017c33a0909d33f1c526714c7ca3b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.matt.2019.05.007"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.                 10.1021/acsenergylett.0c00973</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D1939-1966%26journal%3DACS%2BEnergy%2BLett.%26author%3DJ.%2BM.%2BLee%26author%3DG.%2BSingh%26author%3DW.%2BCha%26author%3DS.%2BKim%26author%3DJ.%2BYi%26author%3DS.-J.%2BHwang%26author%3DA.%2BVinu%26title%3DRecent%2BAdvances%2Bin%2BDeveloping%2BHybrid%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBattery%2BAnodes%26doi%3D10.1021%252Facsenergylett.0c00973&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.3c12984&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Facsenergylett.0c00973&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances in Developing Hybrid Materials for Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lee, Jang Mee; Singh, Gurwinder; Cha, Wangsoo; Kim, Sungho; Yi, Jiabao; Hwang, Seong-Ju; Vinu, Ajayan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">6</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1939-1966</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AELCCP</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2380-8195</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are an attractive prospect for energy storage in applications ranging from portable devices to large-scale energy storage grids.  However, practical application of SIBs is hindered by their large size and concomitant sluggish kinetics.  The hybridization strategy has been widely utilized as one of the effective ways to facilitate the enhancement of battery performance of the SIB electrodes.  A combination of transition metal compds. and carbonaceous species is a popular hybrid that could offer a wide range of possibilities for fabricating high-performance and long-lasting SIB anodes.  This Review specifically highlights hybridization strategies of transition metal compds., including metal oxides, metal sulfides, metal phosphides, and metal carbides with different carbon species.  Systematic discussion of the Na+-ion storage mechanism in these hybrids has been given special importance.  The effect of the hybridization on SIB performance is comprehensively covered in this Review to gauge the full impact of these materials as promising SIB anodic materials for the future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqH0eRa5l__1rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lim1QcSHeL1TQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXpsVCqsLo%253D&amp;md5=a14d975018c81a63eeae1b025dba2b42</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.9b05355"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wahyudi, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Engineering Sodium-Ion Solvation Structure to Stabilize Sodium Anodes: Universal Strategy for Fast-Charging and Safer Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhou, Lin; Cao, Zhen; Zhang, Jiao; Sun, Qujiang; Wu, Yingqiang; Wahyudi, Wandi; Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Wang, Limin; Cavallo, Luigi; Sun, Yang-Kook; Alshareef, Husam N.; Ming, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">20</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are promising alternatives for lithium-ion batteries due to their lower cost caused by global sodium availability.  However, the low Coulombic efficiency (CE) of the sodium metal plating/stripping process represents a serious issue for the Na anode, which hinders achieving a higher energy d.  Herein, it is reported that the Na+ solvation structure, particularly the type and location of the anions, plays a crit. role in detg. the Na anode performance.  It is shown that the low CE results from anion-mediated corrosion, which can be tackled readily through tuning the anion interaction at the electrolyte/anode interface.  The strategy thus enables fast-charging Na-ion and Na-S batteries with a remarkable cycle life.  The presented insights differ from the prevailing interpretation that the failure mechanism mostly results from sodium dendrite growth and/or solid electrolyte interphase formation.  The anionic model introduces a new guideline for improving the electrolytes for metal-ion batteries with a greater energy d.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq1CZ_NcCD96rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh2lEJOHjycFQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXns1ersbY%253D&amp;md5=0e6b3d859b932dc257240456b1ae9bcd</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.0c02049"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Von Lim, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pam, M.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stepwise Intercalation-Conversion-Intercalation Sodiation Mechanism in CuInS2 Prompting Sodium Storage Performance</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yan, Dong; Huang, Shaozhuan; Von Lim, Yew; Fang, Daliang; Shang, Yang; Pam, Mei Er; Yu, Juezhi; Xiong, Dongbin; Li, Xue Liang; Zhang, Jian; Wang, Ye; Pan, Likun; Bai, Ying; Shi, Yumeng; Yang, Hui Ying</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
